♠ Merchant ships pressed into service ♣ inferred absent ♥ On occasion, feuding chieftains would gather fleets: 'Sturla was implacable. Immediately after Snorri's departure he seized Reykholt, took possession of all his belongings, and forced the chieftains of the district to offer their submission. Urökja and Sturla Thordsson, the historian, fled to Aedey, where they began to gather a fleet of ships with which they intended to sail to the Borgafjord, but Sighvat and Sturla harried the seacoast districts and killed many of their adherents.'[MAJIDBENAM_REF_271_IsCommw] 'Thord, however, did not leave Iceland, but fled to some islands, where he gathered a fleet of thirty ships, a naval force which his adversaries did not venture to meet. Kolbein harried the southwestern districts where Thord had received some assistance. He even dispatched a force to Stadarhol to slay Sturla Thordsson. But he had been warned, and they were only able to plunder his estates.'[MAJIDBENAM_REF_272_IsCommw] Gjerset mentions one naval battle: 'Thord now resolved to gather a fleet for an attack on Kolbein, since a march overland to northern Iceland was very difficult. He was able to secure fifteen small vessels, and sailed with a band of 220 men, leaving the defense of the home districts to Sturla Thordsson. But Kolbein, who was informed of his plans, met him with a larger fleet and superior forces in the bay of Hunafloi, and defeated him after a severe engagement.'[MAJIDBENAM_REF_273_IsCommw] [None of these ships/boats appear to have been ocean going merchant vessels. The flagship of the larger fleet in 1244 was ‘almost’ ocean-going. These were large fishing boats, ferries and vessels used for coastal transport each holding about 20-30 men. So probably (inferred) absent.]
♠ Specialized military vessels ♣ absent ♥ [The only recorded naval battle took place in 1244 CE.]
♠ Settlements in a defensive position ♣ inferred present ♥ Amory describes fortifications and strongholds, but says little about the possible strategic location of larger manors: 'Larger groups (sixteen to eighteen members and on up) readily developed a ‘siege mentality’, and would sometimes entrench themselves, not in caves, but in fortified earthworks or strongholds, thrown up against the inroads of their enemies. Both Óspakr and Hörðr had such fortifications built for themselves, their wives and families, and their men, Hörðr's island retreat of wood and turf being virtually impregnable. Though Óspakr's earthworks enclosed a farm with two cows, where his wife and son lived, these fortifications were not internally self-sustaining but were chiefly designed for receiving stolen goods, viz., the produce of the surrounding countryside, and for staging last-ditch defenses. It is remarkable but not unintelligible that the sizeable outlaw colony on the tiny island of Geirshólmr, under the leadership of Hörðr Grímkelsson and his foster-brother Geirr Grímsson, seems never to have engaged in animal husbandry of any sort on the island, or gone fishing in the surrounding waters, but instead preferred to launch expedition after expedition to the mainland in order to rustle from the rich coastal farms the cattle and sheep that it lacked; these would be slaughtered at once for its consumption. One may well think that cattle- and sheep-rustling was a perfectly suitable occupation for outlaws, but they were undercutting themselves by their total dependence on the mainland, and finally allowed themselves to be lured to shore by promises of freedom and were put to death in batches by a coalition of farmers who were lying in wait to dispatch them. So, at any rate, the story of the ‘Hólmverjar’ goes in Harðar saga Grímkelssonar (chs 34ff.). The colony was eradicated in three years' time without the mainland farmers' even having to assault the impregnable hall of Hörðr on one cliffside of the island.'[MAJIDBENAM_REF_274_IsCommw] But it seems likely that the location of manors attached to powerful chieftains and their retainers was chosen with potential attacks in mind. [As far as I know, there are no examples of lords’ residences being deliberately located in defensive positions. They could be located in strategic positions, however, and were often fortified.]
♠ Wooden palisades ♣ inferred absent ♥ [In this case, the building material was stone but the wall was glued together with sot, soil, earth. Most evidence comes from the late 12th century onward. However, it is probable that the data is valid from 930 CE onward. There is evidence of several fortresses and fortified manors. But most or all forts seem to be dry-stone walled (sod probably used between stones). Timber for palisades would have to be imported and thus very expensive and probably less functional than dry-stone walls.]
♠ Earth ramparts ♣ inferred absent ♥ Amory describes fortifications and 'earthworks': 'Larger groups (sixteen to eighteen members and on up) readily developed a ‘siege mentality’, and would sometimes entrench themselves, not in caves, but in fortified earthworks or strongholds, thrown up against the inroads of their enemies. Both Óspakr and Hörðr had such fortifications built for themselves, their wives and families, and their men, Hörðr's island retreat of wood and turf being virtually impregnable. Though Óspakr's earthworks enclosed a farm with two cows, where his wife and son lived, these fortifications were not internally self-sustaining but were chiefly designed for receiving stolen goods, viz., the produce of the surrounding countryside, and for staging last-ditch defenses. It is remarkable but not unintelligible that the sizeable outlaw colony on the tiny island of Geirshólmr, under the leadership of Hörðr Grímkelsson and his foster-brother Geirr Grímsson, seems never to have engaged in animal husbandry of any sort on the island, or gone fishing in the surrounding waters, but instead preferred to launch expedition after expedition to the mainland in order to rustle from the rich coastal farms the cattle and sheep that it lacked; these would be slaughtered at once for its consumption. One may well think that cattle- and sheep-rustling was a perfectly suitable occupation for outlaws, but they were undercutting themselves by their total dependence on the mainland, and finally allowed themselves to be lured to shore by promises of freedom and were put to death in batches by a coalition of farmers who were lying in wait to dispatch them. So, at any rate, the story of the ‘Hólmverjar’ goes in Harðar saga Grímkelssonar (chs 34ff.). The colony was eradicated in three years' time without the mainland farmers' even having to assault the impregnable hall of Hörðr on one cliffside of the island.'[MAJIDBENAM_REF_275_IsCommw] [Fortifications usually seem to be made of stone (and sod) with vertical sides rather than sloping sides as in earth ramparts. It is a theoretical possibility that some such fortifications were made exclusively of sod (this was sometimes done in house-walls) but these would still have vertical sides and seems unlikely as stone is stronger.] We have accordingly chosen to code this as 'inferred absent'.
♠ Ditch ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Moat ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Stone walls (non-mortared) ♣ present ♥ [The walls were non-mortared.]
♠ Stone walls (mortared) ♣ absent ♥
♠ Fortified camps ♣ absent ♥ [According to the codebook this refers to fortified camps of armies on the move. It is highly unlikely that the small warbands of late medieval Iceland would make fortified camps when moving around. In most cases they would overnight at farmsteads.]
♠ Complex fortifications ♣ absent ♥[MAJIDBENAM_REF_276_IsCommw]
♠ Long walls ♣ 0 ♥ in kilometers. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_277_IsCommw]
♠ Modern fortifications ♣ absent ♥[MAJIDBENAM_REF_278_IsCommw]

Military Organization[edit]

♠ Standing armies ♣ absent ♥ [However, the chieftains had some retainers who performed a wide variety of tasks.] Gjerset claims a short period of relative peace for the phase immediately succeeding the introduction of Christianity: 'The introduction of Christianity was followed by a period of peaceful development known as the Fridaröld, 1030-1118. During this period the bloody feuds subsided, churches and schools wer built, and and an intellectual life blossomed forth which elevated Iceland to the position of literary leadership in the North.'[MAJIDBENAM_REF_279_IsCommw] Over time, the godords became concentrated in the hands of a few chieftains who controlled groups of armed followers: 'As armed conflict was still the final argument in so many controversies, the godar were naturally eager to gain possession of new godords whenever possible, in order to increase the number of their thingmenn. How this method was used to satisfy personal ambition at the expense of the existing state organization is clearly seen in the case of the Sturlungs in western Iceland, who rose in this way to the greatest power, though at first they probably did not even possess a godord. The real prominence of this family began with Sturla Thordsson, or Hvamm-Sturla, so called from the estate Hvamm which he had purchased.'[MAJIDBENAM_REF_280_IsCommw] This period saw an intensification of civil strife, mostly in the form of clashes between groups of retainers led by these 'magnates': 'Unlike a modern member of parliament, a chieftain could gain control of more than one godord. In the latter half of the 12th century, there was a growing tendency for more than one godord to belong to the same man. The sagas sometimes mention a godord being given to a man who already had one. Some people who inherited a godord appear not to have wanted it [...], while some proved incapable of providing their liegemen with the protection expected of them. This trend continued, until early in the 13th century the majority of the country had become concentrated in the hands of eight magnates. In addition, some of these were close relatives, so that five families or clans practically ruled the country. At about the same time, conflict intensified. There were more battles, with more numerous forces, and they travelled longer distances to make war. This may have been partly a consequence of the concentration of power: the magnates could raise larger forces of farmers from among their followers, and they had to travel farther to the next magnate's territory. Another contributory factor may have been the influence of the king of Norway. At this time, royal power was in the ascendant in Norway after a prolonged period of discord and civil war. In 1217 King Hakon Hakonson succeeded to the throne; he would reign, largely in peace, until 1263. The king appears to have resolved to gain control of all lands around the North Atlantic inhabited by Norse people. This period of internal strife between magnates, and royal attempts to take control of Iceland, is known in Iceland as the Sturlung Age, in reference to one of the magnate clans. The precise dating of the Sturlung Age varies, but it is logical to date it from when the sources first mention plans by the Norwegian crown to take over Iceland, i.e. 1220. This was when an Icelandic chieftain of the Sturlung clan, Snorri Sturluson, left Norway for Iceand, having undertaken to win Iceland over to the king. Snorri, admittedly, never fulfilled this promise, and one source states that he did not try very hard. But 15 years later another member of the Sturlungs undertook the same task. This was Snorri's nephew, Sturla Sighvatsson. Sturla launched a campaign of attacks on other magnates, aiming to induce them to flee to Norway, where the king would force them to abdicate their powers. But Sturla's campaign came to an end at the Battle of Örlygsstadir in north Iceland, on 21 August 1238. Sturla and his father, Sighvatur, led a force of several hundred, perhaps as many as 1,000, against nearly 1,700 men led by the magnate of the region, Kolbeinn Arnórsson the Young, and Gissur Porvaldsson from the south, a descendant of Gissur the White [...]. Both Sturla and Sighvatur died in battle, with about 50 of their supporters; their opponents only lost seven men. In the following years the conflict continued; among those who died was the chieftain and literary genius Snorri Sturluson, in 1241. In 1258 the king sent Gissur, the victor of Örlygsstadir, home to Iceland, with the tile of jalr (earl) of Iceland. He succeeded in inducing the chieftains and representatives of farmers in teh north, the west and south Iceland to swear allegiance to the king and undertake to pay him taxed in the future. In the next two years the same undertaking was given for the East Fjords and south-eastern Iceland.'[MAJIDBENAM_REF_281_IsCommw] The Sturlung Age was concluded with the establishment of Norwegian sovereignty.
♠ Uniformity of equipment ♣ absent ♥
♠ Total army ♣ [500-1000] ♥ [Armies often numbered about 500-1000 men in the early 13th century, occasionally a bit more but a force of less than 500 would not be a serious contestant in the period 1234-1250. After 1250 armies were usually smaller.] This period saw an intensification of civil strife, mostly in the form of clashes between groups of retainers led by these 'magnates': 'Unlike a modern member of parliament, a chieftain could gain control of more than one godord. His antagonist Haflidi came with no less than 1400 men. [The dispute in question was finally settled through a compromise negotated by the clergy.] The size of the armed bands employed by these chieftains shows that the power had already passed from the godar and the old aristocracy into the hands of a few powerful and ambitious magistrates. [...] Feud was rife everywhere, and more serious clashed occurred from time to time [...].'[MAJIDBENAM_REF_283_IsCommw]
♠ Professional army ♣ absent ♥ [There was no army outside the ad hoc mustered groups of free farmers.] [However, at least after 1200 CE there were also professional bands of retainers, some numbering 40-50 men and totalling 300 men or more.]

♠ Procurement of equipment and animals ♣ by soldiers ♥ [Equipment and animals were mostly provided by the soldiers themselves, but sometimes some equipment was provided by chieftains for their retainers. We know for example that some lords owned many shields.] The code may need to change, but it appears the variable itself will be replaced by new, more precise variable.

General Characteristics of Warfare[edit]

♠ Inter-polity wars ♣ inferred absent ♥ Most clashes were fought between chieftains leading groups of armed retainers: 'Unlike a modern member of parliament, a chieftain could gain contro lof more than one godord. In this struggle the Sturlung brothers stood divided, and became the leaders of hostile factions which they organized in their support. Snorri continued to reside at Reykholt, and took steps to fortify and improve his estate. He surrounded his houses with stone walls, and built also a famous bath, the Snorralaug, of which remnants are still found.'[MAJIDBENAM_REF_291_IsCommw]
♠ Intra-elite conflicts ♣ present ♥ Gjerset claims a short period of relative peace for the period immediately succeeding the introduction of Christianity: 'The introduction of Christianity was followed by a period of peaceful development known as the Fridaröld, 1030-1118. How this method was used to satisfy personal ambition at the expense of the existing state organization is clearly seen in the case of the Sturlungs in western Iceland, who rose in this way to the greatest power, though at first they probably did not even possess a godord. The real prominence of this family began with Sturla Thordsson, or Hvamm-Sturla, so called from the estate Hvamm which he had purchased.'[MAJIDBENAM_REF_293_IsCommw] Most clashes were accordingly fought between chieftains leading groups of armed retainers: 'Unlike a modern member of parliament, a chieftain could gain contro lof more than one godord. In the latter half of the 12th century, there was a growing tendency for more than one godord to belong to the same man. The sagas sometimes mention a godord being given to a man who already had one. Some people who inherited a godord appear not to have wanted it [...], while some proved incapable of providing their liegemen with the protection expected of them. This trend continued, until early in the 13th century the majority of the country had become concentrated in the hands of eight magnates. In addition, some of these were close relatives, so that five families or clans practically ruled the country. At about the same time, conflict intensified. There were more battles, with more numerous forces, and they travelled longer distances to make war. This may have been partly a consequence of the concentration of power: the magnates could raise larger forces of farmers from among their followers, and they had to travel farther to the next magnate's territory. Another contributory factor may have been the influence of the king of Norway. At this time, royal power was in the ascendant in Norway after a prolonged period of discord and civil war. In 1217 King Hakon Hakonson succeeded to the throne; he would reign, largely in peace, until 1263. The king appears to have resolved to gain control of all lands around the North Atlantic inhabited by Norse people. This period of internal strife between magnates, and royal attempts to take control of Iceland, is known in Iceland as the Sturlung Age, in reference to one of the magnate clans. The precise dating of the Sturlung Age varies, but it is logical to date it from when the sources first mention plans by the Norwegian crown to take over Iceland, i.e. 1220. This was when an Icelandic chieftain of the Sturlung clan, Snorri Sturluson, left Norway for Iceand, having undertaken to win Iceland over to the king. Snorri, admittedly, never fulfilled this promise, and one source states that he did not try very hard. But 15 years later another member of the Sturlungs undertook the same task. This was Snorri's nephew, Sturla Sighvatsson. Sturla launched a campaign of attacks on other magnates, aiming to induce them to flee to Norway, where the king would force them to abdicate their powers. But Sturla's campaign came to an end at the Battle of Örlygsstadir in north Iceland, on 21 August 1238. Sturla and his father, Sighvatur, led a force of several hundred, perhaps as many as 1,000, against nearly 1,700 men led by the magnate of the region, Kolbeinn Arnórsson the Young, and Gissur Porvaldsson from the south, a descendant of Gissur the White [...]. Both Sturla and Sighvatur died in battle, with about 50 of their supporters; their opponents only lost seven men. In the following years the conflict continued; among those who died was the chieftain and literary genius Snorri Sturluson, in 1241. In 1258 the king sent Gissur, the victor of Örlygsstadir, home to Iceland, with the tile of jalr (earl) of Iceland. He succeeded in inducing the chieftains and representatives of farmers in teh north, the west and south Iceland to swear allegiance to the king and undertake to pay him taxed in the future. In the next two years the same undertaking was given for the East Fjords and south-eastern Iceland.'[MAJIDBENAM_REF_294_IsCommw] These clashes devolved into a state of civil war: 'After Snorri's return in 1220 the strife waged by rival leaders developed into a civil war involving nearly all parts of the island. In this struggle the Sturlung brothers stood divided, and became the leaders of hostile factions which they organized in their support. Snorri continued to reside at Reykholt, and took steps to fortify and improve his estate. He surrounded his houses with stone walls, and built also a famous bath, the Snorralaug, of which remnants are still found.'[MAJIDBENAM_REF_295_IsCommw] Members of the clergy became involved as well: 'Often the priests themselves would join, sword in hand, in the bloody feuds, like Odd, Lufina and others, only to give the otherwise dark picture of social and political life a still more forbidding aspect.'[MAJIDBENAM_REF_296_IsCommw]
♠ Popular uprisings ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Historical sources prevalently focus on conflicts between elites.
♠ Military revolts ♣ inferred absent ♥ Historical sources prevalently focus on conflicts between elites. The codebook defines "military revolts" as an "[i]nsurrection lead [sic] by a military leader involving components of armed forces". For most other polities, this variable is coded as present when involving elements of those polities' standing armies, but there were none in Iceland at this time (as indicated by expert contribution to the "standing army" variable), it seems most accurate to code this variable as "inferred absent"
♠ Separatist rebellions ♣ absent ♥ The codebook defines "separatist rebellion" is a "[r]ebellion in a region aiming to secede from the center", but there was no such thing as a "center" in Iceland at this time.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_297_IsCommw]
♠ rural succession ♣ absent ♥ The codebook defines "rural succession" is a "[r]ebellion in a ruralarea and/or non-administrative region aiming to secede from the centre", but there was no such thing as a "center" in Iceland at this time.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_298_IsCommw]
♠ Violent civil conflict ♣ suspected unknown ♥ The codebook defines "violent civil conflict" as "[r]iots, demonstrations, and/or other form of rebellion that involves violent confrontation or suppression by authorities", but historical sources prevalently focus on conflicts between elites, that is, between different authorities.
♠ Violent riots urban ♣ suspected unknown ♥ No references to urban riots have been found in the literature.
♠ Oppression / extermination of subaltern group ♣ suspected unknown ♥ No references to the oppression or extermination of a subaltern group have been found in the literature.

♠ Annexation ♣ inferred absent: 930-1150 CE; present: 1151-1262 CE ♥ All evidence stems from the late 12th century and especially the 13th century. The scale of war (army size) in the late Commonwealth was certainly much greater than ever before. However, this primarily reflects the larger and more powerful political units.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Chieftains seized each other's estates when given the chance: 'Sturla was implacable. Immediately after Snorri's departure he seized Reykholt, took possession of all his belongings, and forced the chieftains of the district to offer their submission. Urökja and Sturla Thordsson, the historian, fled to Aedey, where they began to gather a fleet of ships with which they intended to sail to the Borgafjord, but Sighvat and Sturla harried the seacoast districts and killed many of their adherents.'[MAJIDBENAM_REF_300_IsCommw] 'In his victorious career Sturla could not long postpone the final struggle for overlordship in Iceland. Under some pretext he seized the estates of the wealthy Koll Arnason, and marched into southern Iceland with a large force. Gizur Thorvaldsson was summoned to meet him for the purpose, as it was stated, of assisting in the division of Koll's estates, but he was instead made prisoner with his full escort, and had to take an oath that he would leave the country. Sturla also seized many of the estates of the Odda chieftains, the sons of Saemund Jonsson, although he was married to their sister Solveig.'[MAJIDBENAM_REF_301_IsCommw] 'Snorri's nearest male heir at the time of his death was his illegitimate son Urökja. But Gizur seemed to ignore him. He paid him no wergeld for his father, but seized instead the estate Reykholt and gave it to Klaeng Bjarnason, Snorri's stepson. Urökja, who had not forgotten the personal wrongs which he had suffered at Gizur's hands, was the more eager to avenge his father. At the head of an armed force he attacked Reykholt, captured Klaeng Bjarnason and put him to death.'[MAJIDBENAM_REF_302_IsCommw] [Annexation, I believe, refers to taking control of enemy territory rather than the confiscation of his property. Such annexation happened frequently in the late Commonwealth but rarely or ever before say 1190, at least not by force as far as we can tell. However, the territorial lordships took shape as godords ware unified or taken over by an emerging lord but we have no indication of this happening by force until the late Commonwealth. Perhaps this should be time-limited (post 1100? - only one territorial lordship is possibly older).] Accordingly, we have time-limited the code. [Annexation is here used to refer to local lordships but not different polities. Not sure how this fits the intentions of the codebook.]
♠ Looting ♣ inferred present: 930-1150 CE; present: 1151-1262 CE ♥ All evidence stems from the late 12th century and especially the 13th century. The scale of war (army size) in the late Commonwealth was certainly much greater than ever before. However, this primarily reflects the larger and more powerful political units.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Chieftains and their retainers seized their rivals' property and plundered their estates: 'This time Einar and his band went to the home of Ingjald, Sturla's son-in-law, and seized all his cattle. This led to a bloody fight between between the two chieftains at Saelingsdalsheid in 1171, in which Ingjald and many other men fell.'[MAJIDBENAM_REF_303_IsCommw] 'One night they made a most brutal attack on Sturla's home, robbed and plundered, and killed even women and children, but they failed to find Sturla, who happened to be away from home.'[MAJIDBENAM_REF_304_IsCommw] 'Sturla was implacable. Immediately after Snorri's departure he seized Reykholt, took possession of all his belongings, and forced the chieftains of the district to offer their submission. Urökja and Sturla Thordsson, the historian, fled to Aedey, where they began to gather a fleet of ships with which they intended to sail to the Borgafjord, but Sighvat and Sturla harried the seacoast districts and killed many of their adherents.'[MAJIDBENAM_REF_305_IsCommw] 'As soon as he was well enough, he gathered forces for a new expedition to the western districts. On a sudden foray Tumi Sighvatsson, Thord's brother, was taken prisoner and beheaded, Sturla Thordsson's home was plundered, people were killed and property was destroyed throughout the districts.'[MAJIDBENAM_REF_306_IsCommw] They also harried enemy districts: 'Thord, however, did not leave Iceland, but fled to some islands, where he gathered a fleet of thirty ships, a naval force which his adversaries did not venture to meet. Kolbein harried the southwestern districts where Thord had received some assistance. He even dispatched a force to Stadarhol to slay Sturla Thordsson. But he had been warned, and they were only able to plunder his estates.'[MAJIDBENAM_REF_307_IsCommw] Some are reported to have plundered the peasant population of neighbouring districts: 'Snorri's illegitimate son Urökja, a dissolute and violent youth, took possession of the Vatsfjord godord only to continue the vile practices of of his predecessors with increased recklessness. He kept a large band of armed followers, and levied forced contributions upon the neighboring districts for their support. From time to time he would send out armed bands to plunder and ill-treat the people, and seize everything which he might desire, like the arrogant robber knights on the continent.'[MAJIDBENAM_REF_308_IsCommw]

♠ Rape ♣ inferred absent ♥ All evidence stems from the late 12th century and especially the 13th century. However, this primarily reflects the larger and more powerful political units.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Gjerset reports some abductions of women: 'When his turbulent illegitimate son Svein abducted Valgerd, daughter of the priest Olaf Solvason of Helgafell, shortly after he beautiful mother Hallgerd had also been abducted by the chieftain Paul Thordsson, the people were greatly aroused, as Olaf and his brother Paul, the priest at Reykholt, belonged to the most prominent families.'[MAJIDBENAM_REF_316_IsCommw] Rape was apparently not endemic during the Sturlung Age. [Perhaps this should be ‘inferred absent’. There is no mentioning of rape and this may mean that is was actually absent in warfare in Iceland since it was considered a serious crime with heavy repercussions. But it is hard to be sure. Widespread or systematic rape (as required in the codebook) was certainly absent.]
♠ Deportation ♣ inferred absent: 930-1150 CE; absent: 1151-1262 CE ♥ All evidence stems from the late 12th century and especially the 13th century. No evidence could be found in the literature for the deportation or expulsion of the general population, despite the existence of detailed accounts of other consequences of warfare, including the expulsion of leaders.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_317_IsCommw]
♠ Expulsion ♣ inferred absent: 930-1150 CE; absent: 1151-1262 CE ♥ All evidence stems from the late 12th century and especially the 13th century. The scale of war (army size) in the late Commonwealth was certainly much greater than ever before. However, this primarily reflects the larger and more powerful political units. There is evidence on the expulsion of the leaders.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Some leaders were forced to leave the island after defeat: 'With the aid of Thorleif of Gardar he raised an army of 480 men, but Sturla met them with a superior force and defeated them at Baer in a bloody battle. Snorri was not in the battle, but Thorleif of Gardar and Olaf Thordsson, the leaders of the force, were taken prisoners, and were compelled to leave Iceland.'[MAJIDBENAM_REF_318_IsCommw] 'In his victorious career Sturla could not long postpone the final struggle for overlordship in Iceland. Immediately Gizur cut off his retreat and made him prisoner, in spite of the most energetic protest of the bishop and many of his own men. Buth Urökja and Sturla Thordsson the historian were forced to pledge themselves under oath to leave Iceland. Urökja was sent to Norway, but Sturla was later released by Kolbein Ungi, and was allowed to remain at home.'[MAJIDBENAM_REF_320_IsCommw]
♠ Enslavement ♣ inferred absent: 930-1150 CE; absent: 1151-1262 CE ♥ All evidence stems from the late 12th century and especially the 13th century. Sources discussing slavery in Iceland at the time (e.g. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_321_IsCommw]) do not suggest that enslavement was a common consequence of warfare.

♠ Torture ♣ inferred absent: 930-1150 CE; absent: 1151-1262 CE ♥ All evidence stems from the late 12th century and especially the 13th century. The scale of war (army size) in the late Commonwealth was certainly much greater than ever before. However, this primarily reflects the larger and more powerful political units. In the short saga (þáttur) Draumur Þorsteins Síðu-Hallssonar, a slave is tortured after killing his master. This is supposed to have happened in the 11th century and was of course not part of any warfare but it shows that the idea of torture was not unfamiliar. The description of the mutilation of Urökja[MAJIDBENAM_REF_329_IsCommw] also shows elements of torture. However, the codebook allows torture to be coded as having been "present" only if it was widespread or systematic.
♠ Mutilation ♣ inferred absent: 930-1150 CE; absent: 1151-1262 CE ♥ All evidence stems from the late 12th century and especially the 13th century. The scale of war (army size) in the late Commonwealth was certainly much greater than ever before. However, this primarily reflects the larger and more powerful political units.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Some leaders ordered their enemies to be maimed or mutilated after capture: 'But lasting peace could not be concluded, and as Sighvat failed to appear at the conference, evidently according to the secret understanding with Sturla. With incredible treachery Sturla soon broke the agreement, seized Urökja and ordered him to be maimed and blinded. It seems that the men who were to execute this wicked deed yielded only partial obedience to the command, as Urökja was not blinded, but was allowed to escape to Norway.'[MAJIDBENAM_REF_330_IsCommw] 'A minor expedition of his brother Tumi, resulting in the capture of two of Snorro Sturlason's slayers, Simon Knut and Thorstein Gudinason, also gave great encouragement to Thord's friends. Simon was executed, and Thorstein, who admitted that he had inflicted a fatal wound on the great historian, was sentenced to lose his right hand.'[MAJIDBENAM_REF_331_IsCommw] 'As soon as he was well enough, he gathered forces for a new expedition to the western districts. On a sudden foray Tumi Sighvatsson, Thord's brother, was taken prisoner and beheaded, Sturla Thordsson's home was plundered, people were killed and property was destroyed throughout the districts.'[MAJIDBENAM_REF_332_IsCommw] The codebook allows for to be coded as "present" only if it was widespread or systematic. It was used as individual punishment but, judging from the sources, not very frequently, and never systematically in the sense that a group of people were mutilated in the same way (except perhaps when two priests were castrated in 1222).
♠ Targeted massacre ♣ inferred absent: 930-1150 CE; absent: 1151-1262 CE ♥ All evidence stems from the late 12th century and especially the 13th century. Urökja, who had not forgotten the personal wrongs which he had suffered at Gizur's hands, was the more eager to avenge his father. On a sudden foray Tumi Sighvatsson, Thord's brother, was taken prisoner and beheaded, Sturla Thordsson's home was plundered, people were killed and property was destroyed throughout the districts.'[MAJIDBENAM_REF_336_IsCommw] 'With seventy men he secretly proceeded to Reykholt in the night, and gained entrance to Snorri's home while the people were sleeping. Snorri, who was awakend by the noise, attempted to hide in the cellar. Here he was soon discovered, and could easily have been made prisoner, but the treacherous and relentless Gizur sent five men led by his servant Simon Knut to slay the defenseless old man. At the hands of these assassins Snorri lost his life at the age of sixty-three, September 22, 1241.'[MAJIDBENAM_REF_337_IsCommw] On some occasions, unarmed co-residents were massacred along with the head of household: 'One night they made a most brutal attack on Sturla's home, robbed and plundered, and killed even women and children, but they failed to find Sturla, who happened to be away from home.'[MAJIDBENAM_REF_338_IsCommw] 'In the night Eyjolf and his associates with a band of fifty-two armed men made their well-planned attack. They were unable to enter the house, as the doors were barred and the entrances guarded. Finding that their plan of surprise had failed, they set fire to the building, which was soon wrapped in flames. [...] Twenty-five persons found their death in the burning building [...].'[MAJIDBENAM_REF_339_IsCommw] Sometimes, this was extended to the enemy's commoner adherents: 'Sturla was implacable. Immediately after Snorri's departure he seized Reykholt, took possession of all his belongings, and forced the chieftains of the district to offer their submission. Urökja and Sturla Thordsson, the historian, fled to Aedey, where they began to gather a fleet of ships with which they intended to sail to the Borgafjord, but Sighvat and Sturla harried the seacoast districts and killed many of their adherents.'[MAJIDBENAM_REF_340_IsCommw]
♠ General massacre ♣ suspected unknown: 930-1150 CE; absent: 1151-1262 CE ♥ No evidence could be found in the literature for acts that we would classify as a "general massacre", despite the existence of detailed accounts of other consequences of warfare. However, all evidence stems from the late 12th century and especially the 13th century. The scale of war (army size) in the late Commonwealth was certainly much greater than ever before, though this primarily reflects the larger and more powerful political units.

♠ Skeletal evidence of violence ♣ inferred absent ♥ "Little evidence of actual violence has been found on Icelandic skeletal remains from the Viking Age", with the main exception, it seems, of the skeleton of an apparently murdered or executed man at the site Mosfell/Hrísbrú[MAJIDBENAM_REF_341_IsCommw].[MAJIDBENAM_BR]"While the Family Sagas described numerous instances of violence, such as Hallegerd plotting to have her husband murdered, evidence of warfare and violence are scare in the archaeological record. Aside from grave goods, few weapons are found and skeletal remains rarely indicate pathological signs of violence."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_342_IsCommw][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Is this reference relevant (where is Mosfell)?: WALKER, P. et al. List of Wars[edit]

Kingdom of Norway - Sturla's Campaign against Icelandic magnates (1235-1238 CE)[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Kingdom of Norway - Sturling Wars with Icelandic magnates (1238-1255 CE)[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Kingdom of Norway - Conquest of Iceland (1262 CE)[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Icelandic Commonwealth - Conflict amongst the Icelandic chieftains (930-1220 CE)[MAJIDBENAM_BR]A collective ritual is a ritual in which two or more people participate. With the official cult we refer to the set of collective ritual practices that are most closely associated with legitimation of the power structure (including elites, if any).
♠ RA ♣ Eva Brandl ♥
♠ Expert ♣ Axel Kristissen; Arni Daniel ♥Expert comments on specific codes are given in square brackets [ ].[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Note: During the Commonwealth Period, the Icelandic General Assembly formally adopted Christianity. This transition was gradual and not clear-cut. Our codes reflect Christianity as well as some 'traditional' practices that were continued after the formal conversion of the Althing. More information on the process of religious transformation can be found in the relevant text passages below. The following general description was extracted from eHRAF: 'Iceland was settled before any significant impact of Christianity in Scandinavia and the early Icelanders were pagans. Many of the Celtic people incorporated in the foundational population were probably Christian but this appears to have had little impact in the society in general. Pre-Christian religious practices are known largely by poetic and literary sources, all recorded during the Christian era, and some traces of material culture. These sources depict a rich cosmology including the Norse pantheon of gods and giants. Thor held a place of special significance based on his frequent inclusion in person and place names. The Icelanders inhabited an environment rich in supernatural entities including trolls, elves, and ghosts. Prescience and magical abilities were often attributed to individuals. In 1000 A.D., responding to a combination of internal and foreign pressure exerted by the Norwegian king, the Icelanders meeting at the General Assembly decided to adopt Christianity as the common religion. Hencefor th Iceland was officially Christian although many of the traditional beliefs remained.' [MAJIDBENAM_REF_343_IsCommw] 'The political institution of chieftaincy (GOÐORÐ) was rooted in religious function, a priestly office of intermediary between the community and supernatural forces. It is unclear the degree to which the role of chieftains had been secularized by the occupation of Iceland, but it is likely chiefs played a continued role in local religious activities by performing rites and sacrifices. Religious activities were not exclusive to chieftains. Individuals played a variety of intermediary between the mundane and supernatural roles including private devotions, divination, and sorcery. The conversion to Christianity brought with it the institutions of priest and bishop to Iceland. Throughout much of the early period, the institutional power of the church was weak. Churches were located on privately owned farmlands and were built and maintained by the local farmers who maintained a priest or served as priest himself. The early farm churches were small and probably served little more than the household and immediate neighbors.' [MAJIDBENAM_REF_344_IsCommw] 'Pagan ceremonies were varied, and the details handed down by later Christian texts are not clear. Sacrificial rites performed by household heads or chieftains played a part in the ritual. Certain rituals seeking the intercession of spirits for divination or assistance (SEIÐR) were largely performed by women. Special cultic sites or buildings (HOFS) existed but religious ceremonies were not limited to these settings. Sacrificial activities were banned shortly after the conversion and Christian ceremonies such as baptism and communion were introduced.' [MAJIDBENAM_REF_345_IsCommw]

Largest scale collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ Althing Assembly ♥During the Commonwealth period, chieftains, their retainers, and their families congregated in an annual assembly: 'The first permanent settlers brought chieftain-follower and owner-slave relationships, or stratification, with them. There was no equal access to basic resources (Fried 1967). About A.D. 930, the general assembly (Althing), the major institution of Commonwealth Iceland, was established. Each chieftain was obliged to attend its annual meetings. There was no “executive” agency to carry out decisions of the Althing. Most decisions hinged on the amount of force on each side rather than procedural nicety, innocence or guilt, or any concept of justice. The Althing was not primarily a legal institution but an arena for building coalitions, for making, breaking, and testing connections.' [MAJIDBENAM_REF_346_IsCommw]//'Each chieftain had to attend the annual meetings and sit in the lögrétta with two advisors. Apparently few cases were brought to judicial conclusions. Most were arbitrated or settled by violence, if the evidence of the sagas is to be believed (Jones 1935:21). In cases that were adjudicated, plaintiffs had to enforce legal judgments by holding courts of execution at the farms of the defendants. These often led to violent clashes. The annual meetings at the General Assembly functioned more as an arena for building coalitions, for making, breaking, and testing connections than as a legal institution.' [MAJIDBENAM_REF_347_IsCommw]//'There were probably thirty-six godar (sing. godi) or chieftains.' [MAJIDBENAM_REF_348_IsCommw]//'According to the law book Grágás it was the duty of each farmer to serve as þingmaðr for one of the chieftains in the ‘quarter’ (fjórðungr) where he lived. If a farmer became dissatisfied with his chieftain, he was allowed once a year to change his choice. If the chieftain accepted the farmer as his þingmaðr, the farmer had to acknowledge his support for the chieftain in front of witnesses. But not all farmers were entitled to participation in the activities of the assembly, only farmers paying þingtax-þingfararkaupsbœndr. To achieve this status, one had to own a property of a certain size, related to the number of people living on the farm. The chieftain could chose one out of every nine of the þing-tax-paying farmers to follow him to the national Alþing at Þingvellir. Those not chosen to go had to pay an assembly-tax to the chieftain, who in turn paid the men who were to accompany him to Alþing. In return for their support, the chieftain was obliged to represent his þingmenn in all judicial matters. However, it must be admitted that the paragraphs in Grágás which mention the goði-þingmaðr relationship provide few details about what the various parties gained from it or how it was maintained, and basically they give the impression of a weak institution (GR 3: goðar, þingfararkaup, þingmaðr).' [MAJIDBENAM_REF_349_IsCommw]//Gjerset provides a general description of the atmosphere at Althing meetings and the costs incurred by attending chieftains: 'At the opening of the session the thingstead was formally consecrated by the alsherjargodi, a descendant of the first settler, Ingolf Arnarson, who bore that title and had the right to perform this ceremony. From the highest elevation of the Mount of Laws the lögsögumadr would proclaim the laws and decrees. From the same place all announcements to the assembly were also made by persons who for this purpose were allowed to ascend the Mount of Laws. Below was the place of the lögrétta, which no one but the members was allowed to enter. Around the lögrétta and thingmenn a visiting public were assembled. On the plain between the Mount of Laws and the Almannagjá booths, or temporary dwellings, were erected for the members of the thing and those who attended its sessions. These booths were simple four-cornered structures with gabled walls without roofs. Before the opening of the session they were covered with a temporary roof of woollen cloth, the walls were draped with cloth, and the booth was divided into separate chambers for the thingmenn in the same manner. The godi had to attend the Althing unless lawfully prevented, in which case the other godar of his district chose an alternate. If a godi remained absent from the session of the Althing without due cause, he forfeited his godord, so also if he arrived at the thing later than the prescribed time, Thursday evening before sunset. A certain number of thingmenn from the godord had to accompany the godi to the Althing. At the meeting of the várthing he could choose one-sixth of their number either by oral agreement or by lot, but as many could go as wished to attend the thing. Those who were sent as representatives from the godord received board, lodging and free transportation to and from the thing. They were also exempt for the year from paying the thingfararkaup, a tax of ten alnar vadmál assessed by the godi on all the thingmenn who did not attend the Althing.' [MAJIDBENAM_REF_350_IsCommw]//'He had to furnish the animals for the religious sacrifices, he was responsible for the upkeep of the temple, and during the sessions of the Althing he had to provide at his own expense free board and lodgings for his thingmenn at his own booths.' [MAJIDBENAM_REF_351_IsCommw]//'The meeting of the Althing was the great event of the year, not only politically but also socially. All men of means and prominence would go to Thingvellir on that occasion, often accompanied by their wives and daughters, to meet friends and relatives from other settlements, to learn the news from abroad, listen to cases tried in the court, and to take part in the games and amusements. The young men from all parts of Iceland would naturally flock to so eventful a gathering to witness the thing proceedings and indulge in the national sports. Marriages were contracted, as seen from the "Njálssaga" [...]. Expeditions to foreign lands were arranged, and invitations were extended for great feasts at which the conviviality of Icelanders found its fullest expression.' [MAJIDBENAM_REF_352_IsCommw]//The recitation of legal codes figured prominently in the proceedings: 'When the chieftains established the Alþing they also adopted a code of law. Lawspeakers were appointed for three-year terms. Their function was to recite the law orally, one-third at each meeting of the General Assembly, so that the full code would be recited within the term of each lawspeaker. The lawspeaker had the power to change the law, for if no one else reminded him of any item that he omitted to recite, it was dropped from the code.' [MAJIDBENAM_REF_353_IsCommw]//'The highest official in the new general government was the lögsögumadr, who presided over the Althing, proclaimed the new laws and decisions from the Mount of Laws, and recited certain portions of the laws each year before the assembly. He was elected for the term of three years, but by repeated reelections was kept in office for so long a period that his tenure was often equivalent to a life incumbency.' [MAJIDBENAM_REF_354_IsCommw]//'Lög enters many combinations. Lögrétta was the law council (Dennis et al. 1980) or court of legislation (Miller 1990). Rétta means to make right or straight, to stretch out or reach out, to make right or adjust. Literally, lögrétta means something like law righting or repairing. It was composed of each of the chieftains and two advisors for each of them. It elected a lögsögumaðr (literally: law, say, man) or lawspeaker for a three-year term. He was to recite one-third of the law at each annual meeting of the General [Page 87] Assembly. The lögrétta made law, changed law, and declared what the law would be if there were disagreements. A lögkaup was a lawful bargain; a löggarð was a lawful fence; and a löghlið was a lawful gate. A lögbók was a lawbook. Lög attached to something else generally meant something to do with law.' [MAJIDBENAM_REF_355_IsCommw]//'The general assembly could make law and both the Alþingi and local assemblies could hear and judge cases. Verdicts were more influenced by the relative power of the two sides in the case than procedure, concepts of justice, or argument (Jones 1935, 21). Most cases were resolved by violence or arbitration (Miller 1984). The winner won the right to carry out a verdict. Neither the assemblies nor any other body had any power to enforce verdicts. Thus the influence of a verdict from an assembly was only as strong as the winner's power to carry it out. In Hœnsa-Þóris saga the relative strengths of the parties are described and how they fared in their armed confrontations with each other, but not the bases of their arguments or procedures at the assemblies. To the author, the important dimension of the cases at the assemblies was the strength of each side, and he indicates that this determined the verdict. This is quite general, especially in the thirteenth century (Durrenberger unp.).' [MAJIDBENAM_REF_356_IsCommw]//'The decisions rendered in judicial matters could, indeed, be enforced in a general way by the public at large. If a person was fined, outlawed or banished, the people would know how to make the decree effective. But if the guilty person was a powerful chieftain, he might defy both the law and the court, and blood-feud would be resorted to as a final settlement of the dispute. Even the Althing itself, though hedged about with all the inviolability of a sanctuary, became at times the scene of bloody conflicts between angry litigants and their adherents, as no armed force existed which could maintain order and prevent violent outbreaks. Cases of bloodshed and disorder at the thing were indeed exceptional, but several instanced recorded in the sagas show the utter helplessness of the assembly, if the chieftain chose to ignore its decrees and disregard its sanctity.' [MAJIDBENAM_REF_357_IsCommw] [Although Alþingi was not primarily a collective ritual it certainly included such elements both in pagan (one must assume) and Christian times. But Alþingi was not a ritual in itself although it included some ritual elements. We cannot point to a specific ritual at Alþingi that would fit this and so we should probably not include it here.] Accordingly, we may have to decide on another ritual instead.+++++++++++

♠ other ♣ ♥

Most widespread collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ Mass ♥ Possibly services (mass) and other prayers performed at farmstead chapels by families and their co-residents. [In pagan times these would be ‘blót’ (presumably) but in Christian times mass.][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Farmstead chapels were generally small, modest structures: 'The political institution of chieftaincy (GOÐORÐ) was rooted in religious function, a priestly office of intermediary between the community and supernatural forces. It is unclear the degree to which the role of chieftains had been secularized by the occupation of Iceland, but it is likely chiefs played a continued role in local religious activities by performing rites and sacrifices. Religious activities were not exclusive to chieftains. Individuals played a variety of intermediary between the mundane and supernatural roles including private devotions, divination, and sorcery. The conversion to Christianity brought with it the institutions of priest and bishop to Iceland. Throughout much of the early period, the institutional power of the church was weak. Churches were located on privately owned farmlands and were built and maintained by the local farmers who maintained a priest or served as priest himself. The early farm churches were small and probably served little more than the household and immediate neighbors.' [MAJIDBENAM_REF_423_IsCommw]//'Not many of the colonists in Iceland who had learned to know Christianity had accepted it as a distinct worship. Usually they were so little informed as to the Christian doctrine that they regarded Christ as only as a god among other gods, as did Helgi Magri, of whom it is said that he was much mixed in his faith. He named his home Kristnes in honor of Christ, but though he was nominally a baptized Christian, he prayed to Thor when he was in distress or in danger on the sea. His sons Ingjald and Hrolf seem to have been wholly pagan, as they built temples and sacrificed to the heathen gods. Even those who called themselves Christians could not read; they had no priests to instruct them or to preach the gospel to them. In the midst of heathen surroundings they were able only to cling as well as they could to the outward symbols of Christianity. When they built a church they placed a simple cross on the altar, possibly also a few candles, as the structure, undoubtedly, was windowless and dark. Before this altar they knelt to do reverence to the cross, as they had seen Christians do in Ireland and elsewhere, and they would repeat the name of Christ or some favorite saint, which must have been in the main their church service. It was a dim light this Christianity which they knew, but it was the light of the world, which brought with it new moral and social ideals. They knew that Christianity taught charity, forgiveness, purity of life, principles wholly wanting in the old faith, and the new ideals attracted them and touched their hearts. [...] For want of aid and encouragement the early Christians in Iceland gradually lapsed into the paganism which surrounded them, but the influence of their faith helped to pave the way for the missionaries who came to preach Christianity a hundred years later.' [MAJIDBENAM_REF_424_IsCommw]//'The lack of efficient organization which gradually enabled lawless violence to undermine the state was felt to some extent also in the church. Moral laxity and open disregard for the most fundamental Christian teachings were everywhere apparent. The common clergy continued to be a distinctly inferior type, and disciplinary authority was so feebly exercised that prevailing practices often deviated sharply from established church polity. Celibacy of the clergy had not been introduced in Iceland. The choise of bishops, which after the Second Lateran Council in 1139 had been left principally to the cathedral chapters, was here exercised by the Althing with the advice and assistance of the bishops, an arrangement which virtually placed the hcoiceof bishops in the hands of the aristocracy. Priests would often assume the management of cases brought before the Althing, and took so active a part in the bitter quarrels that they were in danger of becoming hostile partisans rather than spiritual advisers and preachers of the gospel. A stricter general supervision was begun in 1152, when Iceland was joined to the new archdiocese of Nildaros in Norway. The gifted and powerful Norwegian archbishop Eystein Erlendsson (1157-1188) inaugurated a policy of reform. He sharpened church discipline, and sought to eradicate flagrant abuses. But even he was able to accomplish but little in the face of the insolent opposition of the chieftains. In letters to the Icelandic bishops he vigorously assailed the prevailing violence and immorality.' [MAJIDBENAM_REF_425_IsCommw] [The main Christian ritual is a weekly mass on Sunday and it usually takes place in a church but sometimes in a chapel. In pagan times this would be some kind of ‘blót’ but not much is known about them.]

Most euphoric collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ Feasts ♥ [There is little or no date on euphoric elements. Assume the same to apply in Iceland as elsewhere in Christian Europe. Unknown for pagan times.] Accordingly, the following codes and descriptions are tentative and provisional at best. We have left them for the time being, but changes may be expedient. Possibly feasts held on joyous occasions, such as weddings. Feasting practiced in domestic settings had pre-Christrian roots: 'Feasting was central to Norse ceremonials and its context was mostly domestic, occurring within the house. The debris of feasting is thus something that archeologists regularly recover when excavating farmstead middens. Yet the everyday menu of rural Norse farmers would not have included freshly slaughtered domestic animals. Sheep and cattle were slaughtered on special occasions of sacrifice and feasting, producing exceptional quantities of discarded animal bone. Rather than treating faunal assemblages as deriving from "domestic" and "mundane" household events, archeologists must recognise that large quantities of animal bone are likely to be the residues of ritual and ceremonial events (Parker Pearson 2000, 2003).' [MAJIDBENAM_REF_481_IsCommw]//Gjerset provides a general description of the atmosphere and proceedings: 'That the spirit of hospitality and love of festive social entertainment which always characterized the Norsemen should continue to flourish in Iceland is natural. This old custom not only gave zest to life in an otherwise bleak and comfortless environment, but it developed grace and skill in conversation, and fostered in the stern and apathetic popular character the traits of generous kindness which continue to distinguish the Icelandic people. Festivities of various kinds were of common occurrence especially in the leading families. During pagan times the yearly religious festivals were the vetrnoetr, or beginning of winter (October 14), the midsvetrar-blót (January 12), and sumardagr, or first day of summer (April 14), when sacrificial feasts usually took place in the temples accompanied by general social pastime. Weddings and funerals were always elaborately celebrated. Occasionally, too, the godi and his retinue had to be entertained for several days when he made his tour through the district, and private parties were often arranged for a large number of invited guests. On such occasions the walls of the stofa were draped with woven tapestries, fire was kindled on the fireplace, and cushions were placed on the benches for the guests. Great quantities of food were provided, as the tables had to display liberality and plenty. Great care had to be taken to place the guests according to rank and dignity at the proper distance to the right and left of the high-seat, since mistakes on this point might lead to serious misunderstandings. The women occupied the pallr, or thverpallr, a bench extending from one wall to the other toward the rear end of the hall. But at the drinking feast which followed the meal men and women would often sit together in couples, and as mead and beer were served in abundance, overindulgence in these strong beverages was not uncommon. Over the mead cup the spirit of conviviality gained full sway. Songs were declaimed, sagas were recited, or sharp irritating word-duels were indulged in, which often ended in quarrels and bloodshed. At greater festivals it was customary for young men to empty the bragarfull, or promise cup, and to to make a solemn vow to do some notable deed. At the termination of the feast, which often lasted several days, the host would give the guests presents, and accompany them on the way, where the final formal leave was taken. The number of guests at weddings, funerals and other festive gatherings was often very large.' [MAJIDBENAM_REF_482_IsCommw]//Feasts could be very costly, especially when the hosts were influential families: 'Costly mantles were often given as presents by chieftains who wished to show their munificence and desired to do a friend special honor (...] The custom of giving costly presents, like scarlet mantles, saddle horses or large sums of money, was quite universally practiced throughout Europe in the Middle Ages, and conformed, no doubt, to the conception of a liberality worthy of princes and men of high station.' [MAJIDBENAM_REF_483_IsCommw]//Dancing and singing may have been introduced during the Commonwealth Period: 'The dance seems to have been unknown in early ages, but it was introduced from the Scandinavian countries, where it developed in connection with romantic folk-songs in the twelfth century, and it soon became a popular public amusement. Men and women would join hands in a large circle, and would move with rhythmic steps to the chanting of some favorite ballad. This pastime was in all its features a distinctly new departure. Before the introduction of Christianity even singing was unknown except in magic incantations, and music, which was limited to the simplest instruments, the harp, the flute and trumpet, was little practiced. These new social features of foreign origin, music and the dance, as well as the singing of romantic love songs, could gain entrance only in the face of adverse public sentiment, but they heralded a time when the chief interest should be transferred from the duelling ground to the drawing room, when the old martial spirit should be tempered with new social ideals.' [MAJIDBENAM_REF_484_IsCommw]//'Few scaldic songs have been preserved in complete form. For the most part only fragments are found as quotations in later prose works, so that, according to Finnur Jonsson, not over a one-hundredth part of the old scaldic poetry has been preserved. These fragments deal principally with historical events, the warlike achievements of the kings and chieftains in whose honor the songs have been written. But it is clear that the interest of the scalds was not limited to this general themes, though the laudatory martial verses give the general tone and character to scaldic poetry. Not infrequently did the scalds devote their entire poems to quite different themes, as did Egill Skallagrimsson in his "Sonatorrek" and Ulf Uggason in his "Húsdrápa". Lovesongs, though condemned by the social etiquette of those times, were nevertheless composed by mane noted scalds like Kormak Önundsson, Thormod Kolbrunarskald and Björn Hitdaelakappi. Often the scalds would encourage their hearers to wisdom, moderation, justice, faithfulness and other manly virtues. They taught the young men what virtues were to be admired and what vices would bring dishonour and contempt. At times they were able also to strike more tender chords which gave expression to the deepest emotions. Their art projected itself into every field and stirred the national spirit to daring and adventure. It became a conventional mode of public utterance, giving expression to the people's thoughts and sentiments -a force so great that even kings would bow before it in silence.' [MAJIDBENAM_REF_485_IsCommw]

Most dysphoric collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ duelling ♥ [There is little or no data on dysphoric elements. Assume the same to apply in Iceland as elsewhere in Christian Europe. Unknown for pagan times.] Accordingly, the following information is tentative and provisional at best. We have left the following material for the time being, but it might need changing. There are two possible rituals who can qualify as the most dysphoric ritual during the Icelandic Commonwealth. The first option is to code for the highly formal and ritualistic form of duelling. Evidence is however relatively circumstantial as we only have sagas referring to these duels and only for the period 930 CE to 1100 CE. For the period 1100 CE to 1262 CE there is no evidence (although it is unsure whether this meant that the tradition had stopped). Duels took place to settle personal dispute. Risk of death was very real, but death was not always the outcome as one side could ask to be spared during the duel. The second option is to code for the tradition to eat bereserk mushrooms and then to go on a rampage. However, as absolutely nothing more is know, we will code here for the duel. According to Gjerset, competitive games and mock fights were a popular pastime and often escalated into violent quarrels: 'These new social features of foreign origin, music and the dance, as well as the singing of romantic love songs, could gain entrance only in the face of adverse public sentiment, but they heralded a time when the chief interest should be transferred from the dueling ground to the drawing room, when the old martial spirit should be tempered with new social ideals. According to the old conception all amusements were to subserve the one prime purpose fo strenghtening the body and training the mind for feats of arms by developing physical skill, courage and martial spirit. Swimming, hunting,skating, ski-running, wrestling, jumping and training in the use of weapons were regarded as the proper pastime for young men, as bravery was the virtue most to be desired. The same spirit also pervaded the distinctly national sports, the playing of ball (knattleikr) and horse-fighting. The game of ball was evidently very different from its present form, thought the details of the game are not known. Two men were pitted against each other. A wooden ball was thrown by one of the players and struck by the other with a wooden mallet. Strength and agility rather than skill decided the outcome, and the temper of the players was often so aroused that the game developed into a violent personal encounter.' [MAJIDBENAM_REF_511_IsCommw] 'Horse-fights seem to have been an especially favorite pastime maong hte chieftains, who raised and trained horses for this purpose.' [MAJIDBENAM_REF_512_IsCommw] 'The contest was, undoubtedly, viewed as a most successful event. Not only the strength of the horses, but the courage and fighting spirit of the men were put to a test whouch aroused the greatest excitement. It was a carnival of conflict as well suited to awaken the martial joy of the spectatirs as te shivering of lances on the blood-stained lists of England and the continent.' [MAJIDBENAM_REF_513_IsCommw] [Duelling should probably not be incuded here. Duelling had some ritualistic elements but was primarily a way of settling disputes.] We have provisionally left the material, but might need to replace it in the future.++++++++++++

There seems to have been a difference between an informal single combat (einvígi) and a formal duel (hólmganga) (Kormáks saga Ch. 10) Sometimes there are assistants that hold shields for the participants and they occasionally take turn exchanging blows (Kormáks saga , Ch. 10; Gunnlaugs saga, Ch. 11). According to Egils saga (ch. 66) a sacrificial bull was presented to be slaughtered by the victor. The best description of the formalities of a duel is in Kormáks saga (Ch. 10). However, none of this can be considered reliable.]

♠ ruling elite ♣ present ♥ "In Commonwealth Iceland there was a system of extraction based on claims to ownership of property, on concepts of the unproblematic [Page 161] differential access to resources in favour of a chieftainly class. The chieftains were unwilling to subordinate themselves to state institutions to protect their privileged positions. The consequence was stratification without a state, the contradiction of an economic system based on property relationships without a congruent institutional system to enforce them. Ownership was as sound as the force one could muster to defend it. There was a complex system of law, but it was all just so much labyrinthine rhetoric in the face of the stark reality that power decided. As slavery diminished, claimants to land enlarged their holdings by using wage labour and tenancy arrangements to work them. To support their claims, they had to increase their power by enlarging their entourages." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_514_IsCommw]
♠ non-ruling elite ♣ present ♥ "Chieftains derived much of their authority from their ability to broker support as advocates for their constituents in legal disputes or feuds.' [MAJIDBENAM_REF_515_IsCommw]
♠ general population ♣ present ♥ "All independent farmers had to be affiliated with a chieftain, although they could choose among any of the chieftains in their quarter and could switch allegiances if they did not feel that their needs were being met. Other than a seat on the parliament, chieftains had few rights beyond those of other independent farmers and few institutional means of dominating others. Chieftains derived much of their authority from their ability to broker support as advocates for their constituents in legal disputes or feuds.' [MAJIDBENAM_REF_516_IsCommw]

♠ Gender ♣ yes ♥ Women in the Icelandic Commonwealth had the final word on accepting an offer of marriage, had relatively easy access to divorce, were often seen encouraging men to take violent action (though they were not allowed to bear arms themselves), controlled large households in their husband’s frequent absences, and were admired equally for courage, honour and strong will. Female representation in Viking heidnitru configured a very different king of gender theology to the patriarchal Christianity that succeeded it in 1000 CE. Rights differ, but the binary is not extreme. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_519_IsCommw] Women could be householders. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_520_IsCommw]
