Deification of Rulers[edit]

♠ Rulers are legitimated by gods ♣ absent ♥ Chieftains derived much of their authority from their ability to broker support as advocates for their constituents in legal disputes or feuds.' [MAJIDBENAM_REF_536_IsCommw] The eminent political institutions of Commonwealth Iceland were the chieftaincy and its associated courts and assemblies: 'At the time of Iceland’s settlement, Norse people worshiped gods whom they called æsir (singular áss), and this religion left behind an extensive mythology in Icelandic literature. Thor seems to have been the most popular of the pagan gods in Iceland, although Odin is thought to have been the highest in rank. It appears that heathen worship was organized around a distinct class of chieftains called godar (singular godi), of which there were about 40. In the absence of royal power in Iceland, the godar were to form the ruling class in the country. By the end of the settlement period, a general Icelandic assembly, called the Althing, had been established and was held at midsummer on a site that came to be called Thingvellir. This assembly consisted of a law council (lögrétta), in which the godar made and amended the laws, and a system of courts of justice, in which householders, nominated by the godar, acted on the panels of judges. At the local level, three godar usually held a joint assembly in late spring at which a local court operated, again with judges nominated by the godar. All farmers were legally obliged to belong to a chieftaincy (godord) but theoretically were free to change their allegiance from one godi to another; the godar were allotted a corresponding right to expel a follower. Some scholars have seen in this arrangement a resemblance to the franchise in modern societies. On the other hand, there was no central authority to ensure that the farmers would be able to exercise their right in a democratic way. No one was vested with executive power over the country as a whole. In any case, no trace of democratic practice reached farther down the social scale than to the heads of farming households; women and workers (free or enslaved) had no role in the political system.' [MAJIDBENAM_REF_537_IsCommw] 'One of the peculiarities of early Iceland was the lack of formal state institutions. The legislature, extensive law code, and judicial system of local and higher courts left prosecution and the enforcement of settlements in the hands of individuals. From an early date, the country was divided into Quarters. Each quarter constituted a broad community with three assemblies (ÞINGS), with the exception of the Northern Quarter that had four, and a system of local courts. Once a year the General Assembly (ALÞINGI) met in the southwest of Iceland. Judicial cases that could not be resolved in local quarters were heard and the parliament (LÖGRÉTTA) convened. The parliament was the principal legislative institution and was responsible for the introduction and maintanence of law. It consisted of chieftains (GOÐAR) from the local quarters. After the conversion to Christianity, the two Icelandic bishops were each given a seat in the parliament. The institution of chieftaincy (GOÐORÐ) was the main locus of political leadership in the country. Originally there were 36 but this number was later expanded. Chieftaincies themselves were a form of property and could be alienated and even divided among multiple individuals. In some cases, individuals asserted power beyond the scope of the political system and controlled multiple chieftaincies. All independent farmers had to be affiliated with a chieftain, although they could choose among any of the chieftains in their quarter and could switch allegiances if they did not feel that their needs were being met. Other than a seat on the parliament, chieftains had few rights beyond those of other independent farmers and few institutional means of dominating others. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_538_IsCommw] [Is ‘ruler’ only the head of the entire polity or do the chieftains and lords in Iceland qualify? In the former sense there was no ruler but in the latter we would still say absent in all cases except perhaps descendant of gods.]
♠ Rulers control supernatural energy ♣ absent ♥ During the pagan period, chiefs frequently fulfilled religious functions: 'Pagan ceremonies were varied, and the details handed down by later Christian texts are not clear. Sacrificial rites performed by household heads or chieftains played a part in the ritual. Certain rituals seeking the intercession of spirits for divination or assistance (SEIÐR) were largely performed by women. Special cultic sites or buildings (HOFS) existed but religious ceremonies were not limited to these settings. Sacrificial activities were banned shortly after the conversion and Christian ceremonies such as baptism and communion were introduced.' [MAJIDBENAM_REF_539_IsCommw]
♠ Rulers are descendants of gods ♣ absent ♥ [However, the genealogies of many leading Icelanders of the 12-13th centuries were traced back to pagan deities, especially Odin. But Odin was no longer considered a god by that time. Nor does this seem to have been important.]
♠ Rulers become gods after death ♣ absent ♥ [However, the genealogies of many leading Icelanders of the 12-13th centuries were traced back to pagan deities, especially Odin. But Odin was no longer considered a god by that time. Nor does this seem to have been important.]
♠ Automatic deification ♣ absent ♥ [However, the genealogies of many leading Icelanders of the 12-13th centuries were traced back to pagan deities, especially Odin. But Odin was no longer considered a god by that time. Nor does this seem to have been important.]
♠ Rulers are gods ♣ absent ♥ [However, the genealogies of many leading Icelanders of the 12-13th centuries were traced back to pagan deities, especially Odin. But Odin was no longer considered a god by that time. Nor does this seem to have been important.]

Normative Ideological Aspects of Equity and Prosociality[edit]

♠ Ideological reinforcement of equality ♣ unknown: 930-1000 CE; present: 1001-1262 CE ♥ [Inferring ideology about equality is difficult and will not be attempted here. However a general Christian ideology can be assumed after 1000 or at least after 1100.] Jesus' message "envisages a universal society bound together by divine love in which the limited human ties of affection based on kinship, cultural identity, and self-interest give way to the unlimited love of God. It calls for an egalitarian kingdom of love without limits. Jesus likens it to a family in which all are brothers and sisters of one another and children of the one Father (‘Abba’, an informal word for father, is Jesus’ preferred name for God)." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_540_IsCommw] [In the eyes of the Christian God, all men were equal but here on Earth they certainly weren’t. On Earth society was divided according to status and social estates. The standard estates in medieval Europe were labourers, fighters and priests. However, according to Christian ideology these estates were supposed to work together and for each other as parts of an organic whole. Rígsþula (probably from around 1000 AD) presumably expressed the ideology of the Nordic elite (not necessarily commoners) and it took a different view where the different estates (in this case, slaves, farmers and nobles) are seen almost as different species. A very interesting discussion of the transformation from one system to another is found in Bagge, S. (2000). Old Norse Theories of Society. From Rígsþula to Konungs skuggsiá. Speculum regale. Der altnorwegische Kdnicjsspiecjel in der europdischen Tradition, JE Schnall et R. Simek éd., Vienne, 8.]
♠ Ideological thought equates rulers and commoners ♣ absent ♥ B on comparison with other Christian societies: "According to a long-standing, and not infrequently contested ideal, European society was composed of a series of hierarchically arranged social groups (estates, orders, and corps), each with a prescribed function and corresponding degree of honour and privileges. In its simplest form, society consisted of three basic groups: the First Estate, the clergy, who prayed; the Second Estate, the nobility, who fought; and the Third Estate, the common people, who worked. This hierarchy of superiority and inferiority was, according to some theorists of the period, inscribed in the order of the universe, so that the terrestrial human hierarchy participated in a greater, divinely sanctioned celestial hierarchy." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_541_IsCommw]
♠ Ideological thought equates elites and commoners ♣ unknown: 930-1000 CE; inferred absent: 1001-1262 CE ♥ Based on comparison with other Christian societies: "According to a long-standing, and not infrequently contested ideal, European society was composed of a series of hierarchically arranged social groups (estates, orders, and corps), each with a prescribed function and corresponding degree of honour and privileges. In its simplest form, society consisted of three basic groups: the First Estate, the clergy, who prayed; the Second Estate, the nobility, who fought; and the Third Estate, the common people, who worked. This hierarchy of superiority and inferiority was, according to some theorists of the period, inscribed in the order of the universe, so that the terrestrial human hierarchy participated in a greater, divinely sanctioned celestial hierarchy." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_542_IsCommw] "Anybody working on early modern churches will be aware of the great significance attached to the correct seating order by early modern men and women ostensibly all 'sharing space' in church. Pews and their arrangement reflected the prevailing social rank of a person and his or her family within this community and therefore were not to be trifled with." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_543_IsCommw] [In the eyes of the Christian God, all men were equal but here on Earth they certainly weren’t. On Earth society was divided according to status and social estates. The standard estates in medieval Europe were labourers, fighters and priests. However, according to Christian ideology these estates were supposed to work together and for each other as parts of an organic whole. Rígsþula (probably from around 1000 AD) presumably expressed the ideology of the Nordic elite (not necessarily commoners) and it took a different view where the different estates (in this case, slaves, farmers and nobles) are seen almost as different species. A very interesting discussion of the transformation from one system to another is found in Bagge, S. (2000). Old Norse Theories of Society. From Rígsþula to Konungs skuggsiá. Speculum regale. Der altnorwegische Kdnicjsspiecjel in der europdischen Tradition, JE Schnall et R. Simek éd., Vienne, 8.]
♠ Ideological thought equates all ethnicities ♣ absent ♥ “The Christian Bible says little about race. Romans, Egyptians, Babylonians, and Jews— all members of a common Mediterranean world— are distinguished primarily by their religious beliefs and cultural practices rather than their color. The few biblical episodes that do mention color were exploited to the fullest in both medieval commentaries on the texts and the medieval Christian imagination. [...]Despite the differences among these commentators, a few consistent points emerge over time. Physical blackness is linked with the lower classes and with those living in sunny climates, but on a spiritual or allegorical level it also indicates a pre-Christian or sinful state. Crucially, the “black but beautiful” formulation, as opposed to “black and beautiful,” becomes ingrained in the discourse of the Christian West. [...]While the Bible may not say much about race, by the close of the fifteenth century there was an established literary and theological discourse surrounding the few black biblical figures. Moses’s Ethiopian wife, Noah’s son Ham, the bride in the Song of Songs, and the queen of Sheba each played some role, small or large, in an increasingly precolonialist rhetoric. 65 Following the Flood, blackness was associated with a particular branch of mankind, the sons of Ham, who were cursed for his sin with their black skin. Black women, not unlike white women, were objects of exchange between men, but their blackness made them unsuitable consorts, often requiring a “whitening” either metaphorically through conversion or by outright erasure. Most significantly, when used as a symbol for Christ or as a path to salvation, the black body ceased to signify a corporeal existence, laying the necessary groundwork for the rapidly approaching Middle Passage slave trade.” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_544_IsCommw] [Icelandic law did distinguish between Icelanders, other Nordics and the rest of the world but this was a legal practicality and there is little or no evidence that any Christians were considered inherently lesser than others. However, there was some ethnic rivalries, e.g. between Norse (Norðmenn, which could include Icelanders) and Swedes. Icelanders were sometimes mockingly called ‘mörlandar’ in Norway (from ‘mör’, the animal fat used to make sausages). Pagans were considered lesser men but could remedy this by becoming Christians (as did the ancestors of the medieval Icelanders). There may have been some prejudice against the Sami people of northern Scandinavia; they were often associated (negatively) with magic. The indigenous people of Greenland and North America were called ‘skrælingjar’, a term that seems to be derogatory. Black people (‘blámenn’ - blue men) are mentioned in some sagas but had become mythical and almost monstrous humans of superior strength (similar to berserkers).]
♠ Ideological thought equates men and women ♣ inferred absent: 930-1000 CE; inferred absent: 1001-1262 CE ♥ “Every era has its own beliefs about what constitutes “women,” “men,” and their proper interrelationship; in the Middle Ages these assumptions accorded rather more authority to religion and less, for example, to science. Thinking on gender was rooted in the three religions of the time, Christianity, Judaism, and Islam; in scientific teachings; and in political traditions. Each constituted an authoritative literate discourse, and together they spoke about women and gender in a remarkably coherent chorus. Women were understood as less than men, female attributes as less good than male ones, and gender relations as properly characterized by womanly submission and manly governance. Theology had pride of place in these discussions not only because so much medieval learning was produced in religious contexts but also because religious inquiry then included almost all knowledge.” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_545_IsCommw] [Should be ‘absent’ throughout. The relative status of women to men was variable but lesser in practically all pre-modern societies. Women may have had a relatively high status in Nordic society but certainly not equal to men.]
♠ Ideological thought equates people of all locations ♣ ♥ [See ethnicities. There was only one resident ethnicity in Iceland. As for locations within Iceland, there was some rivalry but nothing to suggest that some were considered lesser people than others.]
♠ Ideological thought distinguishes rulers and commoners ♣ present ♥ Based on comparison with other Christian societies: "According to a long-standing, and not infrequently contested ideal, European society was composed of a series of hierarchically arranged social groups (estates, orders, and corps), each with a prescribed function and corresponding degree of honour and privileges. In its simplest form, society consisted of three basic groups: the First Estate, the clergy, who prayed; the Second Estate, the nobility, who fought; and the Third Estate, the common people, who worked. This hierarchy of superiority and inferiority was, according to some theorists of the period, inscribed in the order of the universe, so that the terrestrial human hierarchy participated in a greater, divinely sanctioned celestial hierarchy." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_546_IsCommw]
♠ Ideological thought distinguishes elites and commoners ♣ present ♥ Based on comparison with other Christian societies: "According to a long-standing, and not infrequently contested ideal, European society was composed of a series of hierarchically arranged social groups (estates, orders, and corps), each with a prescribed function and corresponding degree of honour and privileges. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_547_IsCommw] "Anybody working on early modern churches will be aware of the great significance attached to the correct seating order by early modern men and women ostensibly all 'sharing space' in church. Pews and their arrangement reflected the prevailing social rank of a person and his or her family within this community and therefore were not to be trifled with." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_548_IsCommw]
♠ Ideological thought distinguishes all ethnicities ♣ present ♥ “The Christian Bible says little about race. Romans, Egyptians, Babylonians, and Jews— all members of a common Mediterranean world— are distinguished primarily by their religious beliefs and cultural practices rather than their color. The few biblical episodes that do mention color were exploited to the fullest in both medieval commentaries on the texts and the medieval Christian imagination. [...]Despite the differences among these commentators, a few consistent points emerge over time. Physical blackness is linked with the lower classes and with those living in sunny climates, but on a spiritual or allegorical level it also indicates a pre-Christian or sinful state. Crucially, the “black but beautiful” formulation, as opposed to “black and beautiful,” becomes ingrained in the discourse of the Christian West. [...]While the Bible may not say much about race, by the close of the fifteenth century there was an established literary and theological discourse surrounding the few black biblical figures. Moses’s Ethiopian wife, Noah’s son Ham, the bride in the Song of Songs, and the queen of Sheba each played some role, small or large, in an increasingly precolonialist rhetoric. 65 Following the Flood, blackness was associated with a particular branch of mankind, the sons of Ham, who were cursed for his sin with their black skin. Black women, not unlike white women, were objects of exchange between men, but their blackness made them unsuitable consorts, often requiring a “whitening” either metaphorically through conversion or by outright erasure. Most significantly, when used as a symbol for Christ or as a path to salvation, the black body ceased to signify a corporeal existence, laying the necessary groundwork for the rapidly approaching Middle Passage slave trade.” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_549_IsCommw]

♠ Ideological reinforcement of inequality ♣ present ♥ Jesus' message "envisages a universal society bound together by divine love in which the limited human ties of affection based on kinship, cultural identity, and self-interest give way to the unlimited love of God. It calls for an egalitarian kingdom of love without limits. Jesus likens it to a family in which all are brothers and sisters of one another and children of the one Father (‘Abba’, an informal word for father, is Jesus’ preferred name for God)." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_551_IsCommw] [I would say present throughout. Rígsþula indicates ideological reinforcement of inequality in pagan times as do the three estates in Christian times. Of course, Christian doctrine also reinforces equality to some extent and there may also have been conflicting pagan ideologies.]
♠ Ideological reinforcement of monogamy ♣ unknown: 930-1000 CE; present: 1001-1262 CE ♥ “The heart of the sacramental argument against real polygamy was that it betrayed the model of marriage between Christ and his church as an exclusive and enduring dyadic union. [...] [Polygamy] was a violation not only of the natural form of marriage, but even more of the supernatural norm which it embodied. For a Christian to pretend to add a second spouse while the rst was still living (with him or her) and to call that new union a marriage was a deliberate insult to Christ’s example and instruction. For a Christian believer or of cial to extend the de nition of marriage to include a second or third concurrent marriage was a deliberate insult to the sacramental symbolism of Christian marriage. It was tantamount to downing a beer and a sandwich and calling that the eucharist, or diving into a pond several times and calling that baptism writ large. This simply could not be. It was not only a “legal impossibility,” as Raymond of Penyafort had put it, echoing Roman law,130 but a grave spiritual offense, too. Medieval writers labeled such misuses of the concept and institution of marriage by Christians as “heresy,” “blasphemy,” and “desecration” - an insult to God and his sacraments. The Council of Trent later called it “heresy” deserving of the church’s “anathema.” “ [MAJIDBENAM_REF_552_IsCommw]
♠ Ideology reinforces prosociality ♣ inferred present: 930-1000 CE; present: 1001-1262 CE ♥ Almsgiving and charity are key aspects of Christian practice [MAJIDBENAM_REF_553_IsCommw].
♠ charity ♣ present ♥ Almsgiving and charity are key aspects of Christian practice [MAJIDBENAM_REF_554_IsCommw].
♠ production of public goods ♣ present ♥ [Bridges and ferries for example.]
♠ communal dining ♣ present ♥ [In pagan times ‘blót’ were presumably communal feast. Feasting was also important in Christian times.]

Normative Ideological Aspects of Human Sacrifice[edit]

♠ Human sacrifice ♣ [4-5]: 930-1000 CE; 0: 1001-1262 CE ♥ Human sacrifices only offered in crises, e.g. famine. “A verse attributed to Thorvaldr Veili contains a message from the author to the poet Ulfr Uggason urging Ulfr to drive ‘the evil pervert and detractor of gods (argan godvarg) over the cliff’ which was precisely the method of offering human sacrifice.” (120, 130) [MAJIDBENAM_REF_555_IsCommw] Despite textual evidence for human sacrifice in Iceland, the practice does not seem to have been altogether common. Sacrificed humans in burials have not been found in Iceland. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_556_IsCommw] “The [Old Testament] expressly abhors and forbids human sacrifice (Lev. 18:21; 20:2-5; Deut. 12:31-32; 18:10; 1 Kgs. 16:30-34). In particular, sacrificing children is seen as an atrocity and an offense against God’s commands (e.g. Jer. 7:31; Ezek. 16:20-21; 20:31).” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_557_IsCommw] "HS seems to have been present in Iceland before Christianity. There are references in written sources to practices of human sacrifice, especially related to famines and old age. This is told in tales related to the Christening of Iceland. These tales both tell of children, poor people and old people sacrificed during times of famine, in order to soothe the anger of gods, which presumably caused the famines." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_558_IsCommw]
♠ human sacrifice of an out-group member ♣ inferred absent: 930-1000 CE; absent: 1001-1262 CE ♥ “The [Old Testament] expressly abhors and forbids human sacrifice (Lev. 18:21; 20:2-5; Deut. 12:31-32; 18:10; 1 Kgs. 16:30-34). Normative Ideological precepts concerning morality/supernatural beings[edit]

Supernatural beings (e.g., gods, spirits, ancestors)
♠ Number of officially sanctioned supernatural beings ♣ polytheistic: 930-1055 CE; monotheistic: 1056-1262 CE ♥ "The whole pantheon of Old Norse divinities is organized like human society: there are husbands and wives, sons and daughters, brothers and sisters."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_576_IsCommw] There is only one Christian god.
♠ Omnipresent supernatural beings ♣ suspected unknown: 930-1055 CE; present: 1056-1262 CE ♥ The fact that, in myth, Odin is often shown acquiring "wisdom"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_577_IsCommw]--including knowledge of the future--could imply either that he was omniscient, because he had acquired the wisdom he sought, or that he was not, because he was often seeking it out. It is not very clear whether Norse gods could be said to have been omnipresent, in the sense that they saw everything but did not necessarily read minds. The Christian god is an omnipresent, omniscient and omnipotent moralizing creator god.
♠ Omniscient supernatural beings ♣ suspected unknown: 930-1055 CE; present: 1056-1262 CE ♥ It is unclear whether Norse gods were omniscient, in the sense that they knew all, including people's thoughts. The fact that, in myth, Odin is often shown acquiring "wisdom"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_578_IsCommw]--including knowledge of the future--could imply either that he was omniscient, because he had acquired the wisdom he sought, or that he was not, because he was often seeking it out. In Christianity: “God exists without beginning or end, as the creator of the universe, omniscient and omnipotent.” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_579_IsCommw]
♠ Omnipotent supernatural beings ♣ inferred present: 930-1055 CE; present: 1056-1262 CE ♥ Norse religion is relatively poorly understood, especially as much of what we know about Norse beliefs generally was recorded by Christian authors. However, perhaps we could infer that Norse gods actively intervened in human affairs (but not necessarily on a moral basis) based on sources such as the poem Hindluljod, where Odin is thus described: “He gives speech and brains to men, sailing wind to men and poetry to poets, courage to many a warrior.”[MAJIDBENAM_REF_580_IsCommw] In Christianity: “God exists without beginning or end, as the creator of the universe, omniscient and omnipotent.” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_581_IsCommw]
♠ High Gods (creator gods) ♣ active: 930-1055 CE; moralizing: 1056-1262 CE ♥ Norse religion is relatively poorly understood, especially as much of what we know about Norse beliefs generally was recorded by Christian authors. However, perhaps we could infer that Norse gods actively intervened in human affairs (but not necessarily on a moral basis) based on sources such as the poem Hindluljod, where Odin is thus described: “He gives speech and brains to men, sailing wind to men and poetry to poets, courage to many a warrior.”[MAJIDBENAM_REF_582_IsCommw] Also, perhaps we could infer supernatural enforcement against harm based on the fact that the Prose Edda--and specifically the Voluspa section--mentions the bodies of oath breakers among the things chewed by the dragon Nidhogg during Ragnarok, the Norse Apocalypse[MAJIDBENAM_REF_583_IsCommw]. Christianity was introduced during the Commonwealth period. The Christian god is "the creator of the universe, omniscient and omnipotent"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_584_IsCommw] and he punishes moral transgression: "[A]fter the Resurrection, when God and Jesus appear to pass judgement, one must 'render an account' (Matt. 12:36). Death ends the day during which good works are to be done, and then, 'the night cometh, when no man can work' (John 9:4). Everyone's work will be tried in fire and revealed (1 Cor, 3:12-13). The just, whom God will gather to His right, receive 'life everlasting', the merciless, however, who stand to His left, 'shall go into everlasting punishment' (Matt. 25:46). Everything is decided with death and that gives both life and death the weight of finality."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_585_IsCommw][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Supernatural Enforcement of Morality
♠ supernatural enforcement against harm ♣ inferred present: 930-1055 CE; inferred present: 1056-1262 CE ♥ Norse morality is relatively poorly understood, especially as much of what we know about Norse beliefs generally was recorded by Christian authors. However, perhaps we could infer supernatural enforcement against harm based on the fact that the Prose Edda--and specifically the Voluspa section--mentions the bodies of murderers among the things chewed by the dragon Nidhogg during Ragnarok, the Norse Apocalypse[MAJIDBENAM_REF_586_IsCommw]. In the Old Testament, "Some sins punishable by death were: murder (Ex 21.12-14), striking or cursing parents (21.15, 17), kidnapping (Ex 21.16), adultery, incest, homosexuality, and bestiality (Lv 20.10-16). Other sins, serious though punishable by death, were: stealing (Ex 21.37-22.3), slandering one's wife (Dt 22.13-19), seducing a virgin (Ex 22.15-16), etc. [...] Effects of Sin. Guilt. As a result of revolt against God, a sense of guilt arose."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_587_IsCommw] "Guilt is the state or condition of being at fault (reatus culpae) and so deprived of supernatural life, the absence of the splendor of which is a stain (macula peccati) on the soul, and also the state or condition of being liable to the penalty due in punishment for the fault."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_588_IsCommw] It's worth noting, however, that it's not very clear how much of Christian morality was adopted by Icelanders at this time, and in what form.
♠ supernatural enforcement of fairness ♣ suspected unknown: 930-1055 CE; inferred present: 1056-1262 CE ♥ Norse morality is relatively poorly understood, especially as much of what we know about Norse beliefs generally was recorded by Christian authors. Christianity: "For example, the Gospels attach the promise of a reward to love of enemies (Mt 5.46), almsgiving (Mt 6.1), forgiveness (Mt 6.15), fasting (Mt 6.18), faith (Lk 12.8), perseverance in the face of persecution (Mt 13.18), and following Christ (Mt 11.29)."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_589_IsCommw] It's worth noting, however, that it's not very clear how much of Christian morality was adopted by Icelanders at this time, and in what form.

In the Old Testament, "Some sins punishable by death were: murder (Ex 21.12-14), striking or cursing parents (21.15, 17), kidnapping (Ex 21.16), adultery, incest, homosexuality, and bestiality (Lv 20.10-16). Other sins, serious though punishable by death, were: stealing (Ex 21.37-22.3), slandering one's wife (Dt 22.13-19), seducing a virgin (Ex 22.15-16), etc. [...] Effects of Sin. Guilt. As a result of revolt against God, a sense of guilt arose."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_595_IsCommw] "Guilt is the state or condition of being at fault (reatus culpae) and so deprived of supernatural life, the absence of the splendor of which is a stain (macula peccati) on the soul, and also the state or condition of being liable to the penalty due in punishment for the fault."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_596_IsCommw] It's worth noting, however, that it's not very clear how much of Christian morality was adopted by Icelanders at this time, and in what form.

Christianity: "[A]fter the Resurrection, when God and Jesus appear to pass judgement, one must 'render an account' (Matt. 12:36). Death ends the day during which good works are to be done, and then, 'the night cometh, when no man can work' (John 9:4). Everyone's work will be tried in fire and revealed (1 Cor, 3:12-13). The just, whom God will gather to His right, receive 'life everlasting', the merciless, however, who stand to His left, 'shall go into everlasting punishment' (Matt. 25:46). Everything is decided with death and that gives both life and death the weight of finality."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_603_IsCommw] It's worth noting, however, that it's not very clear how much of Christian morality was adopted by Icelanders at this time, and in what form.
♠ other supernatural enforcement ♣ inferred present ♥ Norse morality: "Writing in Gylfaginning , Snorri alludes to Vafthrúdnismál, stanza 23, which personifies the sun and moon. But Snorri has a somewhat different and more elaborate story: Mundilfoeri is a man who had two children who were so beautiful that he named them Máni and Sól (i.e., Moon and Sun), and he married Sól to a man called Glen. Thereafter the gods punished this act of pride by placing the children in heaven to serve the actual heavenly bodies, which the gods had created. Sól drives the horses that pull the sun, and Máni controls the motion of the moon and its waxings and wanings. [...] The implication that the act of pride was marrying his daughter to Glen, and not naming one’s children Sun and Moon, is right there in Snorri, but like most people I prefer to ignore it and think that the gods punished Mundilfoeri for the names and not the marriage. There is certainly no evidence that Sól’s marriage to Glen annoyed the gods, for indeed there is no other evidence whatever about this marriage."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_604_IsCommw] Christianity: perseverance in the face of persecution. "For example, the Gospels attach the promise of a reward to love of enemies (Mt 5.46), almsgiving (Mt 6.1), forgiveness (Mt 6.15), fasting (Mt 6.18), faith (Lk 12.8), perseverance in the face of persecution (Mt 13.18), and following Christ (Mt 11.29)."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_605_IsCommw] It's worth noting, however, that it's not very clear how much of Christian morality was adopted by Icelanders at this time, and in what form.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Human Enforcement of Morality

Public Goods[edit]

♠ Famine relief ♣ present ♥ “The essential function of a hreppr was to provide relief for the poor as well as to prevent people from reaching the stage of such poverty.” Care of the poor was primarily the responsibility of relatives, but in some instances the responsibility was extended beyond kinship. In the even that no individual could be held legally responsible for the care of a pauper, and if the pauper was unable to obtain help by submitting to bondage, the necessary relief had to come from the hreppr of his assembly district. An indignant could claim relief from a hreppr district if he had no relatives closer than second cousins living there. After the conversion to Christianity, the hreppr gradually built up enough revenue to support the needy. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_606_IsCommw]

♠ prestige of voluntary religious associations ♣ ♥ [The “lowest social status of people who belong” is at minimum, “lower than top nobility”. This seems absurd as it conflates anything from average nobility to farmhands or even slaves.] [This still makes little sense, even with this explanation in the codebook: “We define 'elite' as the top social, political, economic, and/or religious members of society. In the codes below, when we say to code as 'equal to top nobility', we are referring to the society's elite groups and families. 'Higher than top nobility', then, refers to the ruling family, and 'lower than top nobility' refers to all people at a lower status than the elite groups and families.” How is top nobility different from other nobility? And is the ‘elite’ all of the upper class or just a part of it. Upper classes in historical and modern societies usually number about 1-2% of the population. Is this what is meant by ‘top nobility’ and then why top (there usually isn’t any nobility in the remaining 99%)? However, one can normally differentiate between two layers of the upper class: a gentry (or similar) that makes up the bulk of the upper class population and is mostly important locally and in the polity as a group, and then the high nobility, a much more select group where people are important to the polity as individuals. The prestige of the coisterfolk would have been similar to that of the gentry, abbots a bit higher.]

Taxation[edit]

♠ Degree of taxation ♣ ♥ With the Tithe Law, the tax law enacted in 1096, the ownership of churches became an important source of wealth and power. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_610_IsCommw] [MAJIDBENAM_BR]Taxes are imposed on:
♠ Land ♣ present ♥
♠ Property ♣ present ♥ The tithe law provided that tax paying farmers would contribute 1% of the assessed value of their property each year to be divided into four parts: one for the church, one for the bishops, one for the clergy, and one for the poor. The church owners received the shares for the churches and clergy, and, as most were chieftains who were most influential in the Alþing, they benefited as much as anyone from the tax. Furthermore, lands that belonged to a church were not taxable, so some landowners donated their land to the church and maintained control of it as managers and benefited doubly. This process aided the concentration of land into few hands, and some cite this process as a major cause of the demise of the Commonwealth (Jóhannesson 1974:227).' [MAJIDBENAM_REF_611_IsCommw]
♠ People ♣ present ♥
♠ Trade ♣ absent ♥
♠ Income ♣ absent ♥
♠ Production ♣ absent ♥Taxes are imposed by:
♠ executive or ruling elite ♣ absent ♥
♠ local elites ♣ present ♥
♠ state officials ♣ absent ♥ [Unless one consideres the godordsmen as ‘state officials’.] There was no state structure.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Type of taxes paid:
♠ Staple ♣ present ♥
♠ Labor ♣ absent ♥
♠ Currency ♣ unknown ♥

Wages and Costs[edit]

♠ Wages ♣ unknown ♥ [The information may be available but would take some effort to dig upp and evaluate.]
♠ staple cost of principal source of carbohydrates ♣ unknown ♥ [The information may be available but would take some effort to dig upp and evaluate.]
♠ Top fortune ♣ ♥ [In 1224 Snorri Sturluson is reported to have been the wealthiest man in Iceland. We don’t know his wealth with any certainty but the landed estates he controlled must have been valued at least at around 2,000 ‘hundreds’ (about 2% of all landed property in Iceland). Adding other property we can guess his total wealth vas no less then 3,000 hundreds and may easily have been more. A ‘legal’ farm valued at 20 hundreds was considered average but the normal family holding would have been somewhat smaller. Such large estates seem a relatively recent development in Iceland at that time. In the 12th century, landowners holding 200-300 hundreds were considered very wealthy, at least in western Iceland. ]++++++++++
